Evidence for the Permissibility of Sale from Hadith and Ijma'
Citing hadith and ijma' as proof for bay'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi proof for the permissibility of sale (bay') from Hadith and Ijma', and explain how the hadith implies permissibility.

Core Idea
The Prophet's address to merchants without prohibition proves sale is permissible; consensus confirms it.

This lesson examines the foundational evidence for the legality of commercial transactions in Islam. The author of al-Hidayah begins the Book of Sales by citing a hadith and Ijma' to establish that bay' is fundamentally permissible (mubah). Understanding these proofs helps you appreciate the Hanafi approach to deriving rulings from textual and consensus-based sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its chain
Read the Arabic hadith text and its chain of narration. The hadith is from Isma'il ibn Ubayd ibn Rifa'ah from his father from his grandfather, who accompanied the Prophet to the musalla. This is a sahih hadith recorded by Tirmidhi (no. 1230).
The chain indicates a reputable transmission.
2
Analyse the hadith's implication
The Prophet addresses merchants without prohibiting their activity. He warns that merchants will be raised as sinners except those who fear Allah, do good, and are truthful. The very act of addressing them implies trade is permissible; otherwise, the Prophet would have forbidden it. The warning is about conduct, not the occupation itself.
The absence of prohibition (nahy) indicates permissibility (ibahah) in Hanafi usul.
3
Recognise the use of Ijma' as a secondary proof
The author then cites Ijma' (consensus) that sale is permissible, noting that from the time of the Prophet until today, Muslims have agreed on its legality. Ijma' is a binding source in Hanafi law.
Ijma' strengthens the ruling and shows continuous practice.
4
Connect the historical practice of the Prophet and Companions
The author mentions that the Prophet himself engaged in trade for Khadijah before prophethood, and the great Companions (Abu Bakr, Umar, etc.) were traders. This historical evidence supports the permissibility and shows that trade is a noble and permitted profession.
Practice of the Prophet before prophethood is not a legal proof per se but reinforces the ruling.
5
Derive the ruling: sale is mubah
From the hadith (implicit permission), Ijma' (explicit consensus), and historical practice, the Hanafi ruling is that sale (bay') is fundamentally permissible (mubah). It can become wajib or haram depending on circumstances (e.g., selling a lost item to return it vs. selling alcohol).
The default ruling for transactions is permissibility in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حديث: عن اسماعيل بن عبيد بن رفاعه عن ابيه عن جده انه خرج مع النبي الى المصلى فراى الناس يتبايعون فقال يا معشر التجار فاستجابو الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ورفعو اعناقهم وابصار هم اليه فقال ان التجار يبعثون يوم القيامة فجاراً الامن اتقى الله وبر وصدق. حضرت رفاعہ سے روایت ہے کہ میں ایک مرتبہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے ہمراہ عید گاہ کی طرف نکلا تو آپ نے دیکھا کہ لوگ خرید فروخت کر رہے ہیں۔ آپ نے فرمایا اے تاجروں کے گروہ یہ سب رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی طرف متوجہ ہو کر آپ کی بات سننے لگے اور اپنی گرد نہیں اور آنکھیں آپ کی طرف اٹھا ئیں آپ نے فرمایا کہ تا جر قیامت کے دن فاجر نا فرمان گنہ گار اٹھائے جائیں گے ۔ مگر وہ جو اللہ سے ڈرا اور نیکی کی اور بیچ بولتا رہا۔ ( ترندی جاش ۱۲۳۰) ان بد ایات ثلاثہ میں سے پہلی روایت میں تاجروں کو صدقہ کرنے کی ہدایت کی گئی ہے۔ اور دوسری اور تیسری روایت میں بچے اور دیانت دار تاجر کی منقبت اور ستائش کی گئی ہے۔ تینوں روایات تجارت اور بیع کے جواز پر دلالت کرتی ہیں۔ اجماع بھی جواز بیع پر دلالت کرتا ہے۔ کیونکہ عہد رسالت سے لے کر آج تک تمام مسلمان جو از بیچ پر متفق چلے آرہے ہیں۔ چنانچہ ارباب سیر نے تحریر کیا ہے کہ نبوت سے پندرہ سال پہلے آنحضرت صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ام المومنین خدیجہ الکبری کے واسطے تجارت فرمائی ہے۔ اور ا کا بر صحابہ مثلاً صدیق اکبر فاروق اعظم ، عثمان غنی ، عباس ، عبدالرحمن بن عوف رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہم نے تجارت کی ہے ۔
Hadith: From Isma'il ibn Ubayd ibn Rifa'ah from his father from his grandfather that he went out with the Prophet to the musalla and saw people buying and selling. He said: 'O company of merchants!' They responded to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), raising their necks and eyes towards him. He said: 'The merchants will be raised on the Day of Resurrection as sinners, except those who feared Allah, were righteous, and were truthful.' ... These three narrations indicate the permissibility of trade and sale. Consensus also indicates the permissibility of sale, because from the time of the Prophet until today, all Muslims have agreed upon the permissibility of sale. The historians have written that fifteen years before prophethood, the Prophet (peace be upon him) conducted trade for Umm al-Mu'minin Khadijah al-Kubra, and the great Companions such as Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Abbas, and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf (may Allah be pleased with them) also engaged in trade.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu') by establishing the foundational evidence for the permissibility of commercial transactions. The author first presents a hadith from the Tirmidhi collection in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) addresses merchants, warning them of the peril of sin except for those who combine piety, righteousness, and honesty in business. This hadith, though cautionary, implicitly affirms the legality of trade itself. The author then notes that all three narrations mentioned earlier converge on the same point: trade is permissible. Next, he invokes ijma' (consensus) as a second proof, arguing that from the earliest Islamic era to the present, the Muslim community has uniformly accepted the legality of sale. To strengthen this, he cites historical evidence: the Prophet himself engaged in commerce for Khadijah before his prophethood, and the most eminent Companions—Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Abbas, and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf—were all traders. This dual argument from Sunnah and consensus establishes the ruling that bay' (sale) is fundamentally permissible (ja'iz) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
بیع (bay'): The exchange of a commodity for a price with mutual consent, which the author is proving is legally permissible.
اجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Muslim community on the permissibility of sale, used as a secondary proof after the hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited from the hadith for the permissibility of sale?
A) The Prophet ordered everyone to become merchants
B) The Prophet addressed merchants without prohibiting their activity, showing it is permissible
C) The Prophet said all merchants are sinners
D) The Prophet forbade trade on Fridays

Key Takeaway
Sale (bay') is fundamentally permissible (mubah) in the Hanafi school, established by the Prophet's implicit permission in the hadith and the consensus of the Muslim community.

The Wisdom Behind Legislating Sale
Explaining the rationale for permitting sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rationale for the permissibility of sale in Hanafi fiqh and define its legal effect (milk).

Core Idea
Sale is legislated because humans depend on each other for necessities, and it provides a lawful means of exchange.

Why does the Shari'ah permit sale? This passage reveals the underlying wisdom: human beings cannot produce all their needs alone. They must obtain things from others. Since unlawful means are prohibited, Allah has provided the lawful contract of sale. Understanding this teleological basis deepens your appreciation of how fiqh serves human welfare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that no single person produces all life's necessities; everyone needs what others have.
2
Recognize the two options
Obtaining from others can be either halal (lawful) or haram (unlawful); haram is prohibited.
3
Derive the solution
The Shari'ah legislates sale as the permissible mechanism for fulfilling needs lawfully.
4
Define the legal effect
The ruling of sale (hukm) is that it transfers ownership (milk) to the buyer, granting the right to dispose properly.
Milk is the key term; it means lawful ownership with power of disposal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بیع مشروع ہونے کا سب تعلق البقاء المعلوم اللہ تعالی علی وجہ تمہیں ۔ یعنی زندگی جو خدا کو معلوم ہے اس کو اچھے طریقہ پر باقی رکھنا۔ اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بقائے زندگی کے لئے جن چیزوں کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے وہ سب کسی ایک آدمی کے گھر تیار نہیں ہوتیں بلکہ ایک سامان کوئی تیار کرتا ہے ۔ اور دوسرا سامان کوئی تیار کرتا ہے ۔ پس ضروریات زندگی پورا کرنے کے لئے ہر شخص دوسرے کا محتاج ہوگا ۔ اب دوسرے آدمی سے اپنی ضروریات کی چیزوں کو حاصل کرنا حرام طریقہ پر ہو گا یا حلال طریقہ پر ہوگا ۔ حرام طریقہ سے حاصل کرنا تو شرعاً ممنوع ہے۔ اس لئے بیع کے حلال طریقہ کو مشروع کیا گیا ہے۔ بیع کا حکم ملک ہے۔ یعنی مشتری کو تصرف صبیح پر قدرت دینا۔
The reason for the legislation of sale is to preserve the life known to Allah in a good manner. That is, to maintain the life which Allah knows in a good way. The meaning is that all the necessities of life are not produced in a single person's home; rather, one person produces one item and another produces another. So to fulfill life's needs, every person is in need of another. Now, obtaining one's necessary items from another person could be through a forbidden means or a permissible means. Obtaining through forbidden means is legally prohibited. Therefore, the permissible means of sale has been legislated. The ruling of sale is ownership (milk), i.e., giving the buyer the power to dispose of the property properly.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage explains the underlying wisdom (hikmah) behind the legislation of sale. The author argues that human beings are interdependent: no single individual produces all that he needs. Hence, people must exchange goods. Exchange can occur either lawfully (halal) or unlawfully (haram). Since the Shari'ah prohibits unlawful acquisition, it provides the lawful mechanism of sale (bay'). The author then defines the legal effect of sale: it transfers ownership (milk), granting the buyer the right to dispose of the property in a valid manner (tasarruf sahih). This emphasizes that the purpose of sale is to facilitate lawful ownership and use of property for sustenance. Although the text is in Urdu, it mirrors the classical Hanafi argument that necessity (hajah) and mutual benefit justify the permissibility of sale.

Key Terms
ملک (milk): Ownership; the legal consequence of a valid sale is that the buyer acquires ownership of the item.
بیع (bay'): Sale; a contract that transfers ownership of a specific item for a known price.
تصرف صبیح (tasarruf sahih): Valid disposal; the buyer's right to use, sell, or deal with the property lawfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer must pay zakat immediately
B) Ownership (milk) is transferred to the buyer
C) The seller must guarantee the item forever
D) The contract must be in writing

Key Takeaway
Sale is a lawful mechanism rooted in human interdependence; its core legal effect is the transfer of ownership (milk).

Conditions and Rukn of Sale
Listing conditions for validity and defining the rukn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid sale and identify the rukn, distinguishing between condition and pillar.

Core Idea
A sale's validity requires sane parties, valuable deliverable property, and the verbal pillar of offer and acceptance.

A contract of sale is not valid simply because two people agree. Islamic law sets specific conditions and defines a single pillar. This passage from a Hanafi text clarifies exactly what must be present for a sale to be legally sound, and what its essential structure is. Understanding these basic elements is the foundation of all commercial transactions in the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage and list the two conditions (shurut) for validity of sale mentioned by the author. One relates to the contracting party, the other to the subject matter.
2
Define the rukn
Find the sentence that states the rukn (pillar) of sale. Note that it consists of two parts: ijab and qabul.
The rukn is what fundamentally constitutes the contract; without it, the contract does not exist.
3
Understand the term 'ijab'
The passage gives a specific definition: ijab is the speech that is uttered first, regardless of whether from seller or buyer. Grasp this nuance – it is not tied to the role of the parties.
This differs from some other schools that fix ijab to the seller and qabul to the buyer.
4
Recognize the relationship between ijab and qabul
Qabul is defined as the second speech that is related and responsive to the first. Understand that the two must correspond for the contract to be complete.
If the second speech introduces new terms, it is not acceptance but a counter-offer.
5
Apply the conditions and rukn to a scenario
Given a hypothetical sale, check if both conditions are met and whether ijab and qabul occurred. Determine if the sale is valid (sahih) or void (batil).
Omission of a condition or the rukn renders the sale batil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواز بیع کی شرطیں (1) عاقد کا عاقل ہونا (2) مبیع کا مال متقوم اور مقدورا تسلیم ہونا۔ بیج کا رکن ایجاب اور قبول ہے۔ ایجاب وہ کلام ہے جو پہلے بولا جائے خواہ بائع کی طرف سے ہو یا مشتری کی طرف سے ہو۔ اور اس کے متعلق دوسرے کلام کو قبول کہتے ہیں۔
Conditions for the validity of sale: (1) The contracting party must be sane. (2) The commodity must be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and capable of being delivered. The pillar (rukn) of sale is offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). Ijab is the speech that is uttered first, whether from the seller or the buyer, and the subsequent speech related to it is called qabul.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions for a valid sale (shurut al-bay') and its pillar (rukn). The Hanafi school requires two conditions: first, the contracting parties ('aqid) must be rational (a'qil) — meaning they have legal capacity (ahliyyah) and are not minors, insane, or interdicted. Second, the subject matter (mabi') must be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) according to Islamic law, and it must be physically capable of delivery (maqdur al-taslim) — so items like a fish in the sea or a bird in the air cannot be sold because delivery is impossible. The author then defines the rukn of sale as ijab and qabul. Offer (ijab) is the first statement expressing intent to transact, and acceptance (qabul) is the corresponding response. Importantly, the party who makes the first statement, whether buyer or seller, is considered the offeror. This clarification is crucial for determining which statements count as offer and which as acceptance.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is recognized as valuable by Islamic law and can be lawfully traded.
ایجاب (ijab): The first utterance expressing consent to the transaction, regardless of which party utters it.
قبول (qabul): The second utterance that corresponds to and accepts the offer, establishing the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the pillar (rukn) of a sale?
A) Delivery of the item
B) Payment of the price
C) Offer and acceptance
D) Presence of witnesses

Key Takeaway
A valid sale requires two conditions (sane party and valuable deliverable property) and one pillar (offer and acceptance); without these, the sale is void.

Classification of Sales by Legal Validity and Subject Matter
Classifying sale into nafidh, mawquf, fasid, batil, and by mabi' and thaman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of sale by legal validity and classify sales by subject matter and price arrangement.

Core Idea
Sales are classified by validity and subject; each type carries distinct rulings.

Sales in Hanafi fiqh are categorized along three axes: legal effectiveness, the thing exchanged, and the pricing method. This classification is foundational for understanding contracts, preventing riba and gharar, and applying correct rulings. This passage introduces these categories concisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types by legal validity
List them: nafidh (executory), mawquf (suspended), fasid (irregular), batil (void). Each has a distinct legal effect and conditions for validity.
Nafidh and mawquf are valid but differ in immediate enforceability. Fasid can be rectified; batil cannot.
2
Distinguish sales by subject matter (mabi')
Classify as mullaq (good for price), muqayadah (barter), sarf (currency exchange), or salam (forward sale). Know what is exchanged in each.
Salam is an exception to the rule against selling what one does not possess.
3
Classify sales by price (thaman)
Identify whether the price is based on cost-plus (murabahah), cost (tawliyah), discount (wadi'ah), or negotiation (musawamah).
These categories affect transparency and profit calculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بیچ کی اس کی ذات کے اعتبار سے چار قسمیں ہیں، (1) بیع نافذ (۲) بیع موقوف (۳) بیع فاسد ۴) بیع باطل ان چاروں کے احکام ان کے مواقع پر ذکر کئے جائیں گے۔ اور جمیع کے اعتبار سے بھی بیع کی چار قسمیں ہیں ۔ بیع کی مبیع کے اعتبار سے قسمیں 1) بیع مطلق یعنی بیع العین بالشمن ۲ بیع مقایضہ یعنی بیع العين بالعين (۳) بیع صرف یعنی بیع الثمن بالثمن ۴) بیع سلم یعنی بیع الدین بالعین۔ اور شمن کے اعتبار سے بھی چار قسمیں ہیں۔ بیع مرابحہ یعنی شمن اول سے زائد کے عوض بیچنا۔ (۲) بیع تولیہ یعنی شمن اول کے عوض بیچنا (۳) بیع وضیعہ یعنی من اول سے کم کے عوض بیچنا (۴) بیج مساومہ یعنی اس شمن کے عوض بیچنا جس پر عاقدین اتفاق کرلیں۔
Sale, with regard to its essence, has four types: (1) nafidh (executory), (2) mawquf (suspended), (3) fasid (irregular), (4) batil (void). The rulings of these four will be mentioned in their places. Also, with regard to the general [object], sale has four types. Types of sale by subject matter (mabi'): (1) mullaq: exchange of a specific item for a price; (2) muqayadah: exchange of a specific item for another specific item; (3) sarf: exchange of price for price (currency exchange); (4) salam: exchange of a debt for a specific item (forward sale). Also, with regard to the price (thaman), there are four types: (1) murabahah: selling at a price higher than the original cost; (2) tawliyah: selling at the original cost; (3) wadi'ah: selling at a price lower than the original cost; (4) musawamah: selling at a price agreed upon by the parties.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents two classification schemes for sales in the Hanafi school. First, by their legal effectiveness (bi-i'tibar al-dhat): (1) nafidh (executory) — a sale that is immediately binding and valid; (2) mawquf (suspended) — a sale that is valid but depends on consent of a guardian (e.g., by a minor); (3) fasid (irregular) — a sale that is defective in some condition but not fundamentally prohibited; (4) batil (void) — a sale that is fundamentally prohibited and has no legal effect. Second, by what is exchanged (bi-i'tibar al-mabi'): (1) mullaq: cash sale of an item for money; (2) muqayadah: barter; (3) sarf: exchange of money for money (currency transaction); (4) salam: forward sale where full payment is made now for future delivery. Third, by the nature of the price (bi-i'tibar al-thaman): (1) murabahah: cost-plus sale; (2) tawliyah: sale at cost; (3) wadi'ah: sale at a discount; (4) musawamah: negotiated price sale. This classification helps jurists apply the correct legal rulings for each type, especially regarding riba, gharar, and mutual consent.

Key Terms
بیع نافذ (bay' nafidh): An executory sale that is immediately binding and valid.
بیع موقوف (bay' mawquf): A suspended sale whose validity depends on the approval of a guardian or principal; e.g., a minor's sale.
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): An irregular sale that is defective in a condition but the essence is permissible; it can be rectified.
بیع باطل (bay' batil): A void sale that is fundamentally prohibited and has no legal effect.
بیع مطلق (bay' mullaq): A sale of a specific item for a price, typically immediate exchange.
بیع مقایضہ (bay' muqayadah): Barter: exchange of a specific item for another specific item.
بیع صرف (bay' sarf): Currency exchange: sale of one monetary commodity (gold, silver, or modern currency) for another.
بیع سلم (bay' salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance and the goods are delivered later; a special contract to facilitate trade.
بیع مرابحہ (bay' murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit margin.
بیع تولیہ (bay' tawliyah): A sale at the original cost, without profit or loss.
بیع وضیعہ (bay' wadi'ah): A sale at a price lower than the original cost, at a discount.
بیع مساومہ (bay' musawamah): A sale at a price negotiated by the parties, without reference to the cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of sale involves exchanging a specific item for another specific item?
A) Bay' mullaq
B) Bay' muqayadah
C) Bay' sarf
D) Bay' salam

Key Takeaway
Sales are categorized by legal effect, subject matter, and price—each with distinct rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

Formation of Sale: Offer and Acceptance with Past Tense
Explaining that sale is formed by past-tense offer and acceptance with alternative expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that sale contracts require past-tense offer and acceptance in the Hanafi school, and apply the principle that meaning-based expressions are legally effective.

Core Idea
For sale, past tense creates the contract; meaning counts more than wording.

In Hanafi fiqh, the verbal form of offer and acceptance in a sale is critical. The school holds that past-tense verbs (e.g., 'I sold', 'I bought') are required to effect the contract, because sale is the creation of a legal disposition (insha' tasarruf). However, this does not mean only those exact words work; any expression that clearly conveys immediate mutual satisfaction is considered valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the scope of acceptable alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State that sale is concluded by past-tense offer and acceptance. Note the example: 'biat' (I sold) and 'ishtarayt' (I bought).
This is the core ruling.
2
Understand the Rationale
Recognize that sale is an act of insha' (creation), and Shari'ah has designated past-tense forms for this purpose. Past tense is originally for reporting (ikhbar) but is used for creation in contracts.
The distinction between insha' and ikhbar is key.
3
Note the Exception for Future Tense
If one party uses future tense (e.g., 'I will sell'), the contract is not concluded. Contrast this with marriage (nikah), where future tense is also valid.
The difference is explained in the Book of Nikah.
4
Expand to Alternative Expressions
Expressions such as 'raditu' (I am satisfied), 'a'taytu' (I gave), or 'khudh' (take) are valid because they convey the same immediate consent.
Meaning (ma'na) is what matters, not the exact wording.
5
Apply the Principle of Mutual Satisfaction
The contract is concluded for the offering party even if the price is low, as long as mutual satisfaction (taradi) is realized.
This emphasizes the Hanafi focus on genuine consent over formalities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال البيع ينعقد بالايجاب والقبول اذا كانا بلفظى الماضى مثل ان يقول احدهما بعت والآخر اشتريت لان البيع انشاء تصرف والانشاء يعرف بالشرع والموضوع للاخبار قد استعمل فيه فينعقد به ولا ينعقد بلفظين احدهما لفظ المستقبل بخلاف النكاح وقد مر الفرق هناك وقوله رضيت بكذا او اعطيتك بكذا او خُذه بكذا في معنى قوله بعت واشتريت لانه يؤدى معناه والمعنى هو المعتبر في هذه العقود ولهذا ينعقد لعاطي في الـ ـفيس س والخسيس هو الصحيح لتحقق المراضاة
He said: Sale is concluded by offer and acceptance when they are in the past tense, such as one saying 'biat' (I sold) and the other 'ishtarayt' (I bought), because sale is the creation of a disposition (insha' tasarruf), and creation is known by the Shari'ah, and the [form] used for reporting (ikhbar) has been used for creation, so it is concluded by it. And it is not concluded by two words, one of which is in the future tense, contrary to marriage (nikah), and the difference has been mentioned there. And his saying 'raditu bi kadha' (I am satisfied with such) or 'a'taytuka bi kadha' (I gave you such) or 'khudhhu bi kadha' (take it for such) is in the meaning of his saying 'bi't' and 'ishtarayt', because it conveys the same meaning. And the meaning is what is considered in these contracts. For this reason, it is concluded for the giver in the case of [...] and the low value is correct because mutual satisfaction is realized.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a direct quotation from the matn (likely Al-Hidayah or similar) discussing the technical requirements for the formation of a sale contract. The author rules that sale is validly concluded using past-tense verbs for both offer (b'i't - I sold) and acceptance (ishtarayt - I bought). The reasoning: sale is an act of creating a legal disposition (insha' tasarruf), and the Shari'ah has prescribed that past-tense forms, which are originally used for reporting (ikhbar), are employed for creation (insha') as well. Therefore, sale is concluded with such forms. However, if one of the forms is in the future tense (e.g., 'I will sell'), the sale is not concluded, unlike marriage (nikah) where future tense is also effective. The author references the difference explained in the Book of Nikah. He then expands: expressions like 'I am satisfied' (raditu), 'I gave' (a'taytu), or 'take it' (khudh) can substitute for 'I sold' and 'I bought' because they convey the same meaning of mutual consent. The underlying principle is that the meaning (ma'na) is what matters in these contracts. The author concludes by noting that the contract is concluded for the offering party even if the price is low, as long as mutual satisfaction (taradi) is achieved. This emphasizes the Hanafi emphasis on mutual consent over formalities.

Key Terms
انشاء تصرف (insha' tasarruf): The creation of a legal disposition; sale is an act that brings about a new legal relationship.
معتبر (mu'tabar): Considered; in contracts, the meaning (substance) is what is legally considered, not just the wording.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 'I am satisfied' (raditu)
C) 'I might buy it' (conditional)
D) 'I bought it last week' (past narrative)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, sale contracts require past-tense offer and acceptance, but expressions conveying immediate consent are acceptable because meaning prevails over wording.

Types of Sales in Hanafi Fiqh
Introductory taxonomy of sales from three angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and define the four categories of sales from three perspectives: legal effect, subject matter, and pricing method, and distinguish Hanafi fasid from batil.

Core Idea
Sales in Hanafi fiqh are classified by validity, object, and price — each category has distinct rulings.

Before diving into specific rulings, the Hanafi school organizes sales into clear categories. Understanding these categories will help you navigate the detailed rules for each type. This passage introduces three classification schemes that are foundational for the entire chapter on sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three perspectives
Read the passage and note that sales are classified from three angles: legal effect (binding/suspended/invalid/void), subject matter (what is exchanged), and pricing method (how the price is determined).
These perspectives are independent and overlap in practice.
2
List categories by legal effect
From the first set, identify the four types: nāfidh (binding), mawqūf (suspended), fāsid (invalid but correctable), bāṭil (void). Understand the basic meaning of each.
Fāsid and bāṭil are distinct in Hanafi fiqh – this is a key difference from other schools.
3
List categories by subject matter
From the second set, identify the four types: muṭlaq (absolute – thing for price), muqāyaḍah (barter – thing for thing), ṣarf (exchange of currency or gold/silver), salam (deferred delivery – price now for commodity later).
Bayʿ ṣarf has strict rules of immediate possession and equality for same type.
4
List categories by pricing method
From the third set, identify the four types: murābaḥah (sale at profit over cost), tawliyah (sale at cost), waḍīʿah (sale at a loss/discount), musāwamah (sale at negotiated price).
The first three require disclosure of cost; musāwamah does not.
5
Recognize the Hanafi specificity
Note that the Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes fasid from batil. Fasid sales may become valid if the defect is rectified before delivery; batil sales never become valid.
This distinction affects legal consequences such as ownership and liability.

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on sales by categorizing all sales from three perspectives: legal effect (binding, suspended, invalid, void), subject matter (asset for price, asset for asset, currency for currency, debt for asset), and pricing method (profit, cost, discount, negotiation). These are fundamental taxonomies used throughout Hanafi fiqh to determine rulings for each type. The author lists them without proof as an introductory classification.

Key Terms
بیع نافذ (bayʿ nāfidh): A sale that is immediately binding on both parties without any option to rescind.
بیع فاسد (bayʿ fāsid): A sale that is defective due to a missing condition but can be corrected; if consummated, it may become valid with legal consequences.
بیع مطلق (bayʿ muṭlaq): An absolute sale where a specific object is exchanged for a specific price.
بیع صرف (bayʿ ṣarf): Exchange of one currency (or gold/silver) for another, with specific rules on hand-to-hand delivery.
بیع مرابحہ (bayʿ murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost price and adds a known profit.

Formation of Sale Contract: Offer and Acceptance in Past Tense
Ruling with proof on past tense requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a sale contract is valid only when offer and acceptance are expressed in the past tense, explain the proof from the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ), and distinguish when ta'ati (mutual transaction) dispenses with verbal expression.

Core Idea
The past tense is the only verbal form that creates a sale; future tense creates promises, not contracts.

Among the most foundational rules of Islamic commercial law is the requirement that a sale contract be formed by a clear offer and acceptance in the past tense. This lesson from the Hidayah tradition explains why the Shari'ah restricts the language of contract formation to the past tense, drawing on the nature of legal creation (insha') and the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ). Understanding this rule is critical for distinguishing valid contracts from mere negotiations and for appreciating the flexibility of the Hanafi school through the concept of ta'ati (mutual transaction).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the formation of a sale contract (bay'). The ruling is that it is concluded by offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) only when both are expressed in the past tense. Any deviation (e.g., future tense) invalidates the contract unless it falls under ta'ati.
The ruling is 'sahih' (valid) when conditions are met.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two proofs: (1) the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) who used past tense in contracts, and (2) rational reasoning that sale is an act of insha' (creation), and the Shari'ah has adopted the past tense for insha' even though it was originally for news (ikhbar).
The dalil is from Sunnah (ahad) and rational reasoning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof prove the ruling? The Prophet's usage establishes a customary Shari'ah practice. Additionally, the nature of insha' requires a verb form that definitively establishes a new legal reality; the past tense does that, whereas the future tense implies a promise or intention.
The Arabic verb 'ba'tu' (I sold) immediately transfers ownership.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (e.g., from other schools or even within Hanafi) allow the imperfect verb (mudari') if it is intended for the present tense. The author refutes this by explaining that the text says 'future' explicitly, and mudari' without sin/sawfa defaults to future in contract context per Shari'ah.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that past tense is required for offer and acceptance. The exception of ta'ati (mutual transaction) is upheld because mutual satisfaction achieves the same meaning.
The tarjih is based on zahir al-riwayah and the established practice of the Salaf.

میں سے کسی ایک نے فعل مضارع کا تلفظ کیا اور حال کے معنی کا ارادہ کیا تو اس سے بیع منعقد ہو جاتی ہے کیونکہ حال مراد لینے کی صورت میں سابقہ دونوں خرابیوں میں سے کوئی خرابی لازم نہیں آتی نہ وعدہ کی خرابی اور نہ بھاؤ کرنے کی خرابی بلکہ اس صورت میں بیچ محقق اور موجود ہو جاتی ہے۔ اس اشکال کے جواب میں کہا گیا ہے کہ عبارت میں لفظ مستقبل مذکور ہے۔ اور فعل مضارع، مستقبل کے لئے اس وقت خاص ہو گا جبکہ اس پر سین یا سوف داخل ہو۔ اور سین یا سوف کے داخل ہونے کے بعد مضارع حال کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا۔ بلکہ استقبال کے
The legal status of offer and acceptance in sale: with which wordings is sale concluded? He said: Sale is concluded by offer and acceptance when both are in the past tense, such as one says 'I sold' and the other says 'I bought', because sale is an act of creating a disposition, and creation is known by the Shari'ah; the past tense, though originally for informing, has been used for creation in Shari'ah, so it is concluded by it. And it is not concluded by two wordings where one is future tense, contrary to marriage, and the difference has been mentioned there. And his saying 'I am satisfied with this' or 'I give you this' or 'Take this' is in the meaning of 'I sold' and 'I bought' because it conveys the same meaning, and meaning is considered in these contracts; for this reason, sale is concluded by mutual transaction (ta'ati) whether the item is precious or cheap—this is correct because mutual satisfaction is achieved. Explanation: The conclusion of a contract means the speech of one party is legally connected to the speech of the other so that the effect appears in the subject matter, i.e., the sold item leaves the seller's ownership and enters the buyer's ownership. Offer means establishing, because it establishes the option of acceptance for the other; when the other party accepts it, that speech is called acceptance. So the first speech from the parties is offer, the second is acceptance. The ruling is: Sale is realized by offer and acceptance provided both are expressed in the past tense, e.g., one says 'I sold' and the other says 'I bought', then sale is concluded. The proof is that sale is an act of creation, and creation is known by Shari'ah. The past tense, though originally for news, is used in Shari'ah for creation, so sale is concluded by it. Sale is of the type of creation because creation is establishing something that did not exist. This definition applies to sale, because through sale, something non-existent and unestablished is established: before the contract, the buyer did not own the item and the seller did not own the price, but the contract establishes both. So sale is from the category of creation. The principle is that sale should be concluded with a word put for creation, but since the Legislator did not put a specific word for creation, the past tense, which in language is for news but in Shari'ah is used for creation, concludes the sale. The author of al-Hidayah says: Sale is not concluded by two wordings where one is future tense and the other past tense. Some commentators say that 'future' means the imperfect verb (fi'l mudari'): if one party uses mudari' and the other past tense, sale is not concluded, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, used the past tense for concluding sale, not mudari'. Therefore, the conclusion of sale is restricted to the past tense. The second proof: if the mudari' is from the seller, e.g., he says 'I will sell' and the buyer says 'I bought', that is a mere promise from the seller, and a promise does not conclude a sale; rather, it is concluded by words that indicate actual existence. If the mudari' is from the buyer, e.g., the seller says 'I sold' and the buyer says 'I will buy', that is bargaining, not concluding the sale. An objection is raised: the mudari' can indicate the present as well as the future, and the present meaning is real. So if a party uses mudari' intending the present, the sale would be concluded because the previous defects (promise or bargaining) do not apply. The response: the text mentions 'future' and the mudari' becomes specific to future only when 'sawfa' or 'sin' is prefixed; after that, it cannot indicate the present.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the Hidayah commentary. It establishes the fundamental Hanafi rule that a sale contract is validly concluded only when the offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) are stated in the past tense (fi'l madi). The reasoning is that sale is an act of legal creation (insha'), and the Shari'ah has adopted the past tense—originally designed for news—to perform this creation, following the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ). The author explains that past tense words like 'b'itu' (I sold) and 'ishtaraytu' (I bought) create the contract instantly, while future or present tense verbs create only promises or negotiations. An important exception is mutual transaction (ta'ati), where no verbal formula is used but the contract is valid due to mutual satisfaction. The passage also addresses an objection: the mudari' (imperfect verb) can indicate the present tense, so it could be used to conclude a contract. The refutation limits this by noting that in the matn, the term 'future' (mustaqbal) is used, and mudari' is only unambiguously future when prefixed by 'sin' or 'sawfa'; without these, it can indicate present but the default in contract context is future per Shari'ah convention.

Key Terms
ایجاب (ījāb): The first speech from one party establishing the option of acceptance for the other; in sale, normally 'I sold'.
قبول (qabūl): The second speech from the other party expressing agreement to the offer.
انشاء (inshā'): Creating a legal reality that did not exist before, such as transferring ownership through a contract.
تعاطی (ta'āṭī): Mutual transaction where parties exchange goods and price without verbal offer and acceptance, e.g., handing over cash.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a seller says 'I will sell you this for ten' and the buyer says 'I bought', the contract is:
A) Valid because the buyer used past tense
B) Valid because mutual consent exists
C) Not concluded because the seller used future tense
D) Invalid unless witnessed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale contract is valid only when offer and acceptance are both expressed in the past tense, as the past tense is the Shari'ah vehicle for legal creation (insha').

Why Past Tense is Required for Sale but Not for Marriage
Explains tense requirement difference with ta'lil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding tense in sale and marriage, and explain the legal rationale (ta'lil) for the difference.

Core Idea
Rights return to the agent in sale, but to the principal in marriage — that's why sale needs past tense, marriage allows imperative.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi contract law: the requirement of past tense for both offer and acceptance in sale, contrasted with the permissibility of mixed tenses in marriage. The difference is not arbitrary but grounded in the legal consequences of agency: where rights return determines the validity of the contract form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the Hanafi rule for tense in sale and marriage as given in the passage. For sale, both parties must use past tense; for marriage, one may use imperative and the other past.
2
Examine the Examples
Read the examples: if one says 'bi' (sell!) and the other 'bi'tuka' (I sold), the sale fails. If the man says 'zawwijni' (marry me) and the woman says 'zawwajtuka' (I married you), the marriage is valid.
Note that these examples illustrate the rule in practice.
3
Trace the Rationale (Ta'lil)
Understand why: rights in sale return to the agent (wakil), so if one person acts as both seller and buyer via an imperative, he becomes both transferor and recipient — an impossibility. In marriage, rights return to the principal (muwakkil), avoiding this conflict.
The legal cause is the return of rights, not the tense itself.
4
Apply the Principle
Given a scenario where an agent is involved, determine whether the contract is valid by checking where rights return. If it's a sale and one party used an imperative, the contract is invalid.
This principle also explains why a single person cannot be both offeror and acceptor in sale but can in marriage.

Book of Sales. ...becomes specific. So if one of the two contracting parties uses a present tense verb into which seen or sawf enters, due to the previous defects, the sale is not concluded. Some commentators of Hidayah said that the expression of Bidayah: 'in the future tense' means the imperative form. And the context of Hidayah's expression is contrary to marriage. Because the opening expression of the Book of Marriage: 'It is concluded with two words, one expressing the past and the other the future, such as saying: zawwijni' — here 'future' means the imperative form. Now the conclusion is that in a sale, if one speaks in the past tense and the other in the imperative, the sale is not concluded. For example, someone says: 'Ya'ni hadha al-'abda bi-alf — sell me this slave for a thousand.' The other says: 'Bi'tuka — I sold you this slave for a thousand.' This sale is not concluded. Contrary to marriage: if a man addresses a woman saying 'Zawwijni — marry me' and she says 'Zawwajtuka — I married you,' then the marriage is concluded. The reason for the difference is that the word 'zawwijnī' is a term for marriage, and 'bi' is a term for sale. One person can be the guardian (wali) for both sides (offer and acceptance) in marriage, but cannot be the guardian for both sides in sale. The reason is that in sale, rights return to the agent (wakīl), but in marriage, they do not return to the agent; rather they return to the principal (muwakkil). Therefore, in sale, if one person becomes the guardian for both sides, then one person would become the claimant (mutālib) and the one claimed from (muṭālab), and the transferor (mumallik) and the recipient (mutamallik). This is impermissible and impossible. This occurs, for example, if Khālid appoints Hāmid as his agent to sell his book, and Hāmid purchases it himself such that, as agent, he makes the offer (ījāb) on Khālid's behalf, and as principal, he accepts (qabūl) for himself — so both offer and acceptance are from Hāmid. Since rights in sale return to the agent, Hāmid becomes the transferor (as agent-seller) and also the recipient (as buyer), and he claims the price from himself. Thus one person becomes claimant and claimed, transferor and recipient. This all arises from the imperative form. Therefore we say that each contracting party should speak in the past tense. If one uses the past and the other the imperative, the sale is not concluded. In marriage, since rights return to the principal, this problem does not arise. For example, a man appoints a woman as his agent for marriage by saying 'Zawwijīnī — marry me,' and she marries him by saying 'Tazawwajtuka — I married you.' The marriage is valid even though both offer and acceptance are from the woman, because rights return to the principal (the husband). The husband is the claimant of the wife's private parts, the wife is the one claimed from. The husband is the transferor of the dower, the wife is the recipient. Since one person does not become claimant and claimed, transferor and recipient in marriage, a single person is permitted to be guardian for both sides. Therefore, in marriage, one party may use the past tense and the other the imperative.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining why past tense is required in both offer and acceptance for a valid sale, whereas in marriage different tenses may be combined. The commentator expands on a point from the Hidayah, clarifying that the requirement for past tense in sale is rooted in the nature of rights: in sale, rights (like ownership and price claim) return to the agent, so if a person acts as both seller and buyer via an imperative form, he becomes both transferor and recipient, claimant and claimed. To avoid this impossible situation, the sale must be in past tense. In marriage, rights return to the principal, so no conflict arises. The passage provides concrete examples: if one says 'sell me' (imperative) and the other says 'I sold' (past), the sale fails. But if a man says 'marry me' (imperative) and the woman says 'I married you' (past), the marriage is valid. The reasoning is based on maʻnā (legal cause) rather than a direct textual proof, making it a taʻlīl argument.

Key Terms
صیغہ ماضی (ṣīghat māḍī): Past tense verbal form used for offer or acceptance in contracts.
صیغہ امر (ṣīghat amr): Imperative verbal form used for offer, e.g., 'bi' (sell!) or 'zawwij' (marry!).
وکیل (wakīl): Agent authorized to execute a contract on behalf of another.
موکل (muwakkil): Principal who appoints an agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The tense requirement in sale and marriage differs because rights return to the agent in sale but to the principal in marriage, which dictates whether a single person can act as both offeror and acceptor.

Option of Acceptance (Khiyar al-Qabul) in Hanafi Sales
Ruling on the option to accept or reject an offer during the session.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-qabul, explain its rationale (illa), and apply it to scenarios involving direct speech and written/messenger communication.

Core Idea
The offeree's right to accept or reject is limited to the session; the offeror may revoke before acceptance.

This lesson introduces the basic Hanafi rule regarding the option of acceptance (khiyar al-qabul). When one party makes an offer, the other party has the right to accept or reject it within the same meeting (majlis). This rule protects the offeree from being bound without consent and gives the offeror the freedom to revoke before acceptance. We will examine the reasoning, the session limitation, and special cases like letters and messengers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is making an offer (ījāb) in a sale. The ruling: the offeree has the option to accept or reject during the session.
2
Understand the rationale
The option exists because otherwise the offeree would be bound without consent. The offeror may revoke because no right of the offeree has been established.
The text uses 'luzum without consent' and 'no violation of another's right'.
3
Note the time limit
The option lasts only until the end of the session (majlis). The session is treated as one unit to avoid hardship.
For letters and messengers, the session is when the message is received.
4
Recognize the condition of whole acceptance
The offeree cannot accept part of the goods or part of the price unless each part is separately priced, because that would split the transaction without the other's consent.
If separately priced, each part is a separate transaction.

خیار قبول کا حکم
قال واذا اوجب احد المتعاقدين البيع فى الآخر بالخيار ان شاء قبل فى المجلس وان شاء رده وهذا خيار القبول لانه لو لم يثبت له الخيار يلزمه حكم العقد من غير رضاه واذا لم يُقد الحكم بدون قبول الآخر فللموجب ان يرجع لخلوه عن ابطال حق الغير وانما يمتد الى آخر المجلس لان المجلس جامع للمتفرقات فاعتبرت بساعاته ساعة واحدة دفعا للعسر وتحقيقا لليسروالكتاب كالخطاب وكذا الارسال اعتبر مجلس بلوغ الكتاب واداء الرسالة وليس له ان يقبل في بعض المبيع ولا ان يقبل المشترى بعض الثمن لعدم رضاء الآخر بتفرق الصفقة الا اذا بين ثمن كل واحد لانه صفقات معنى
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا اور جب متعاقدین میں سے ایک نے بیج کا ایجاب کیا تو دوسرے کو اختیار ہے کہ اگر چاہے تو اسی مجلس میں قبول کمرے اور اگر چاہے تو اس کو رد کر دے اور یہ خیار قبول ہے۔ کیونکہ اگر اس کو قبولیت کا اختیار حاصل نہ ہو تو بغیر اس کی رضا مندی کے بیچ کا حکم لازم آئے گا۔ اور جب اس ایجاب نے بغیر دوسرے کے قبول کے حکم مبیع کا فائدہ نہ دیا تو ایجاب کر نیوالے کو بھی اپنے ایجاب سے
Ruling on the option of acceptance. He (al-Qudūrī) said: When one of the two contracting parties makes an offer (ījāb) of sale, the other has the option: if he wishes, he may accept in the session, and if he wishes, he may reject it. This is khiyār al-qabūl (option of acceptance). Because if he had no option, the contract would be binding on him without his consent. And since the offer does not create a binding effect without acceptance, the offeror may revoke, because there is no violation of another's right. It extends to the end of the session because the session gathers together things that are separate; its hours are considered as one hour to avoid hardship and achieve ease. A letter is like direct speech; similarly, sending a messenger — the session is considered the time of receiving the letter or hearing the message. He may not accept part of the sold item, nor may the buyer accept part of the price, because the other does not consent to splitting the transaction, unless the price for each part is specified, because then they are multiple transactions in meaning. Translation: al-Qudūrī said: When one of the two contracting parties makes an offer of sale, the other has the option: if he wishes, he may accept in the same session, and if he wishes, he may reject it. This is khiyār al-qabūl. Because if he had no option, the ruling of the contract would bind him without his consent. And when the offer does not produce the effect of sale without the other's acceptance, the offeror may revoke his offer because it does not involve nullifying another's right.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Qudūrī quoted in al-Hidayah, defining khiyār al-qabūl (option of acceptance). It states that after an offer (ījāb) is made, the offeree has the right to accept or reject during the same session (majlis). This prevents binding the offeree without consent. The offeror may also revoke before acceptance because no right of the other party has been established. The option extends to the end of the session; the session's duration is treated as a single unit to facilitate transactions. For communication via letter or messenger, the 'session' is the time when the recipient receives the message. The offeree cannot accept a part of the goods or price unless each part is separately priced, in which case each is an independent transaction.

Key Terms
خیار قبول (khiyār al-qabūl): The option given to the offeree to accept or reject an offer during the session of the contract.
مجلس (majlis): The physical meeting place where the offer is made; for absent parties, the time of receiving communication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, can the offeror revoke his offer before the offeree accepts?
A) Yes, because no right of the offeree has been established yet.
B) No, an offer is binding once made.
C) Only if the offeree agrees.
D) Only before the offeree speaks.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi option of acceptance (khiyar al-qabul) gives the offeree the right to accept or reject during the session, and the offeror may revoke before acceptance.

Khiyar al-Qabul: Option of Acceptance in Sales
Explaining the ruling, proof, and duration of khiyar al-qabul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-qabul, explain its rational proof, and apply the rules regarding the seller's revocation and partial acceptance.

Core Idea
The buyer's option to accept or reject lasts only until the end of the council; the seller may revoke before acceptance because actual ownership outweighs the right to become owner.

In this passage, the commentator of Al-Hidayah explains the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-qabul. You will learn the rational proof for this option, the seller's right to revoke before acceptance, and why partial acceptance is invalid unless each item's price is separately stated. The passage also clarifies the duration of the option using the analogy of the council as a single legal moment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is khiyar al-qabul? It is the buyer's option to accept or reject the offer within the same council. The ruling is that this option is a legal right (mubah) to ensure consent.
The passage establishes the option as necessary for contract validity.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): without the option, the buyer would be forced, and consent—essential for a valid sale—would be lost.
The proof is based on the necessity of mutual consent.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The absence of an option leads to compulsion, which negates consent; therefore, the option is required to preserve consent.
Consent is a core requirement (rukn) of the contract.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objection states that the buyer acquires a right to become owner (haqq al-tamalluk) from the offer. The Hanafi response: actual ownership (haqiqat al-milk) is stronger, so the seller may revoke without invalidating a greater right.
Analogy: father's right vs. child's actual ownership.
5
Apply the rules
The option is limited to the council; the seller can revoke before acceptance; partial acceptance is invalid unless each item's price is specified. Contracts by letter or messenger follow the same principle: the council of receipt governs.
This ensures ease and prevents hardship for both parties.

کتاب البيوع
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم
رجوع کرنیکا اختیار ہے۔ کیونکہ ایجاب کر نیوالے کا ایجاب سے رجوع کرنا ابطال حق غیر سے خالی ہے اور خیار قبول آخر مجلس تک اس لئے دراز ہوتا ہے کہ مجلس متفرق چیزوں کو جمع کر نیوالی ہے پس تختی دور کرنے کے لئے اور آسانی ثابت کرنے کے لئے مجلس کی تمام ساعتیں ایک ساعت شمار ہوئیں اور تحریر خطاب کے مانند ہے اور یہی حکم قاصد بھیجنے کا ہے حتی کہ خط پہنچنے اور پیغام ادا کرنے کی مجلس معتبر ہوگی اور بائع کو یہ اختیار نہیں کہ بعض مبیع میں بیع کو قبول کرے اور نہ یہ کہ مشتری مبیع کو بعض شمن کے عوض قبول کرے کیونکہ معاملہ متفرق ہونے کی وجہ سے دوسر اراضی نہیں ہوگا۔ مگر جب کہ ہر ایک کا شمن بیان کر دیا ہو کیونکہ یہ معنی کئی صفقہ ہیں ۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ جب متعاقدین میں سے کسی ایک نے بیع کا ایجاب کیا مثلاً بائع نے کہا کہ بعتک بذا بکذا‘ میں نے یہ سامان اتنے شمن کے عوض فروخت کیا تو دوسرے کو یعنی مشتری کو مجلس کے اندر اندر قبول کرنے اور رد کرنے کا اختیار ہے یعنی چاہے تو اس بیع کو قبول کرے اور چاہے تو اس کو رد کر دے فقہاء کی اصطلاح میں اس اختیار کا نام خیار قبول ہے۔ اس خیار کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر ایجاب کر نیوالے کے علاوہ دوسرے کو قبول کرنے اور رد کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہو تو وہ ان دونوں میں سے ایک پر مجبور ہوگا اور جب وہ مجبور ہوا تو اس کی رضا مندی فوت ہو گئی حالانکہ انعقاد بیع کیلئے بائع اور مشترکی دونوں کی رضامندی ضروری ہے اس لئے اس کے واسطے خیار قبول حاصل ہوگا اور جب احد المتعاقدین کا ایجاب بغیر قبول آخر کے مفید حکم اور مفید ملک نہ ہوا تو ایجاب کرنے والے کیلئے اختیار ہے کہ وہ قبول آخر سے پہلے پہلے اپنے ایجاب سے رجوع کرے کیونکہ فقط ایجاب سے مشتری کے لئے مبیع کے اندر نہ ملک ثابت ہوتی ہے اور نہ حق ملک ثابت ہوتا ہے پس جب دونوں باتیں نہیں ہیں تو موجب ایجاب کر نیوالے) کے رجوع کرنے سے ابطال حق غیر یعنی مشتری کے حق کو باطل کرنا لازم نہیں آتا اور جب کسی کے حق کو باطل کرنا لازم نہیں آیا۔ تو موجب کا اپنے ایجاب سے رجوع کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔ ایک سال اور اسکا جواب : اس کا یہ سوال ہے وہ ہے کہ ایجاب کرنے سے مشتری کے لئے اگر چہ ملک اور حق ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتے لیکن مشتری کے لئے حق متملک (مالک بننے کا حق ) ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔ یعنی ایجاب بیع سے مشتری کیلئے یہ حق ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔ کہ وہ قبول کر کے مبیع کا مالک ہو جائے پس جب بائع نے اپنے ایجاب سے رجوع کر لیا تو مشتری کا یہ حق باطل ہو جائے گا لہذا یہ کہنا کہ موجب کے رجوع کرنے سے ابطال حق غیر لازم نہیں آتا غلط ہے۔ بلکہ ابطال حق غیر لازم آتا ہے اور جب موجب کے رجوع عن الایجاب سے ابطال حق غیر لازم آتا ہے تو بائع کا اپنے ایجاب سے رجوع کرنا جائز نہ ہونا چاہئے تھا حالانکہ آپ جواز کے قائل ہیں۔
اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ جب فقط ایجاب بیج مفید ملک نہیں ہے تو مبیع کے اندر حقیقت ملک بائع کیلئے ہوئی اور مشتری کے لئے حق تملک ہوا اور حقیقت ملک اقویٰ ہے بہ نسبت حق تملک کے پس اگر بائع کو اپنے ایجاب سے رجوع کا اختیار نہ دیا جائے تو ادنی یعنی حق تملک کی وجہ سے اقومی یعنی بائع کے حق ملک کو باطل کرنالازم آئے گا ۔ اور یہ کونسی دانشمندی ہے کہ ادنی کی رعایت میں اعلیٰ اور اقومی کو باطل کر دیا جائے اس لئے موجب یعنی بائع کو ایجاب سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار دیا گیا ہے۔ اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسے اولاد کے مال میں باپ کو حق تملک حاصل ہے مگر اولاد کیلئے حقیقت ملک ثابت ہے تو باپ کے مالک بننے سے پہلے اولاد کو اس مال میں ہر طرح کے تصرف کرنے کا اختیار ہے یعنی باپ کے حق تملک کی وجہ سے بیٹے کی حقیقت ملک کو نظر انداز نہیں کیا جا سکتا پس اسی طرح مشتری کے حق تملک کی وجہ سے بائع کی حقیقت ملک کو نظر انداز نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ وإنما يمتد الى آخر المجلس..... الخ سے سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ مشتری کے رد اور قبول کے اختیار کو مجلس کے
ساتھ کیوں خاص کیا گیا ہے جواب عقلی طور پر تین صورتیں ہیں ایک تو یہ کہ ایجاب کے فورا بعد قبول کر نالازم قرار دیا جائے اگر فورا قبول نہ کرے تو ایجاب باطل ہو جائے ۔ دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ قبول کرنے کو اور مادران انجلس پر موقوف کیا جائے ۔ یعنی مشتری کو یہ اختیار دیا جائے کہ مجلس میں قبول کرے یا مجلس کے بعد قبول کرے دونوں صورتوں میں بیج تام ہو جائے گی ۔ تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ خیار کو آخر مجلس تک باقی رکھا جائے مجلس ختم ہونے کے بعد خیار قبول باقی نہ رہے ان تینوں صورتوں میں سے پہلی صورت میں مشتری کے لئے دشواری پیدا ہوگی ۔ بایں طور کہ اس کو بلا غور کئے ایجاب کے فوراً بعد جواب دینا پڑے گا غور فکر کا موقع نہیں ملے گا اور دوسری صورت یعنی خیار کو ماوراء مجلس پر موقوف رکھنے کی صورت میں بائع کے لئے دشواری پیدا ہوگی بایں طور کہ اس کو بیچ کے نام ہونے یا نہ ہونے کے سلسلہ میں بہت مدت تک انتظار کرنا پڑ سکتا ہے۔ اور تیسری صورت میں دونوں کے لئے آسانی ہے کیونکہ مشتری کو بھی مجلس کے اندر غور و فکر کر نے کا موقع ملے گا۔ اور بائع کو بھی بہت زیادہ انتظار نہیں کرنا پڑیگا۔ اور مجلس متفرق چیزوں کو جمع بھی کر نیوالی ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے ایک مجلس میں آیت سجدہ کو بار بار پڑھا تو ایک ہی سجدہ واجب ہوگا پس بائع اور مشتری سے تنگی دور کرنے کے لئے اور آسانی کو پیدا کرنے کے لئے مجلس کی تمام ساعتوں کو بمنزلہ ایک ساعت کے اتار لیا گیا اور آخر مجلس تک جواب دینا ایجاب کے فوراً بعد جواب دینے کے مانند ہو گیا۔ اس لئے خیار قبول آخر مجلس تک باقی رہے گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بذریعہ خط عقد بیع کرنا اور پیغام رسانی کے ذریعہ بیع کرنا خطاب کے مانند ہے ۔ حتی کہ خط پہنچنے اور پیغام ادا کرنے کی مجلس معتبر ہوگی ۔ مثلاً ایک شخص نے کسی کو لکھا کہ میں نے اپنا فلاں غلام تجھکو ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض فروخت کیا پس جس مجلس میں اس کو یہ خط ملا اگر اسی مجلس میں اس نے کہا کہ میں نے خرید لیا یا قبول کیا تو بیع نام ہو جائے گی یا مثلاً بائع نے خالد سے کہا کہ میں نے اپنا فلاں غلام حامد کو ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض فروخت کیا تم یہ پیغام حامد کو پہنچا دو پس قاصد جس مجلس میں پیغام لے کر پہنچا اور حامد کوسنایا تو اگر حامد نے اسی مجلس میں اشتریت یا قبلت" کہا تو بیع نام ہو جائے گی ۔ ورنہ نام نہ ہوگی۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بائع یا مشتری کے لئے بعض میچ میں بیع قبول کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہے۔ مثلاً بائع نے کہا کہ میں نے یہ دو چیزیں ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض فروخت کیں تو مشتری کو یہ اختیار نہ ہوگا کہ وہ ان میں سے ایک چیز کو پانچ سوروپیہ کے عوض قبول کرے۔ اس طرح اگر مشتری نے کہا کہ میں نے یہ دو چیزیں ایک ہزار روپے کے عوض خرید میں تو بائع کو یہ اختیار نہ ہوگا کہ وہ ان میں سے ایک چیز کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض قبول کرے اسی طرح مشتری کے لئے اختیار نہیں کہ وہ بیع کو بعض ثمن کے عوض قبول کرے بائع نے کہا کہ میں نے یہ غلام تیرے ہاتھ ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض فروخت کیا مشتری نے کہا کہ میں نے پانچ سو روپیہ کے عوض قبول کیا تو درست نہیں ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ ان صورتوں میں صفقہ متفرق ہو جاتا ہے۔ بایں طور کہ بائع نے غلام بیچا ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض اور مشتری نے اس کو پانچ سو روپیہ کے عوض قبول کیا تو صفقہ بدل گیا اور موجب یعنی بائع صفقہ متفرق کرنے پر راضی نہیں ہے اور تفرق صفقہ پر راضی اس لئے نہیں کہ تفرق صفقہ کی صورت میں موجب کا نقصان ہوتا ہے اس طور پر کہ جب بائع نے کسی چیز کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض فروخت کیا اور مشتری نے پانچ سوروپیہ کے عوض قبول کیا تو اس صورت میں بائع کو پانچ سو روپیہ کا نقصان برداشت کرنا پڑتا ہے۔ یا مثلاً بائع نے دو چیزیں ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض فروخت کیں اور مشتری نے ان میں سے ایک کو پانچ سو روپیہ کے عوض قبول کر لیا تو بائع کا یہ نقصان ہوگا کہ لوگوں کی عادت یہ ہے کہ وہ رڈی اور گھٹیا چیز کو جید کے ساتھ ملا کر فروخت کرتے ہیں اور رڈی چیز کو فروخت کرنے کے لئے جید کی قیمت کو کم کر دیتے
Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume eight. The granter has the option to revoke. Because the granter's revocation of the offer does not involve invalidating the right of another. And khiyar al-qabul is extended to the end of the council because the council is that which brings together different things. So to remove difficulty and establish ease, all moments of the council are counted as one moment. Writing is like direct address, and the same ruling applies to sending a messenger, so that the council of receipt of the letter and delivery of the message is considered. The seller does not have the option to accept the sale for part of the sold item, nor may the buyer accept the sold item in exchange for part of the price, because the transaction would not be consented to due to division, unless the price of each has been stated, because then there are multiple transactions. Explanation: The issue is that when one of the two contracting parties makes an offer of sale, for example the seller says 'I have sold this item to you for such and such price,' then the other, i.e., the buyer, has the option to accept or reject within the council—meaning if he wishes, he accepts the sale, and if he wishes, he rejects it. In the terminology of jurists, this option is called khiyar al-qabul. The proof of this option is that if the other party besides the granter did not have the option to accept or reject, then he would be forced into one of the two, and when he is forced, his consent is lost, whereas for the conclusion of a sale, the consent of both the seller and buyer is necessary; therefore, he has khiyar al-qabul. And since the offer of one of the two contracting parties without subsequent acceptance does not confer a ruling or ownership, the granter has the option to revoke his offer before subsequent acceptance, because from the mere offer, neither ownership nor the right of ownership is established for the buyer in the sold item. So when both matters are absent, the granter's revocation does not necessitate invalidating the right of another, i.e., the buyer's right. And when invalidating someone's right is not necessitated, there is no harm in the granter's revocation of his offer. Objection and its answer: The objection is that from the offer, although ownership and the right of ownership are not established for the buyer, the buyer does have the right of tamalluk (the right to become owner). That is, from the offer of sale, the buyer has this right: he can become owner of the sold item by accepting. So when the seller revokes his offer, this right of the buyer becomes void; therefore, the statement that revocation does not necessitate invalidating the right of another is wrong. Rather, it does necessitate invalidating the right of another. And when revocation necessitates invalidating the right of another, then the seller's revocation of his offer should not be permissible, whereas you hold it permissible. The answer is that when the mere offer of sale does not confer ownership, then actual ownership of the sold item is for the seller, and for the buyer there is the right of tamalluk. Actual ownership is stronger than the right of tamalluk. So if the seller is not given the option to revoke his offer, then due to the lesser, i.e., the right of tamalluk, it would necessitate voiding the greater, i.e., the seller's right of ownership. And what wisdom is there that in consideration of the lesser, the higher and stronger is voided? Therefore, the granter, i.e., the seller, is given the option to revoke his offer. And it is like a father has the right of tamalluk in the property of his child, but for the child, actual ownership is established, so before the father becomes owner, the child has the right to make all kinds of disposition in that property—meaning the child's actual ownership is not ignored due to the father's right of tamalluk. Similarly, the buyer's right of tamalluk does not cause the seller's actual ownership to be ignored. 'And it extends to the end of the council...' etc. is the answer to the question. The question is: Why is the buyer's option to reject and accept specifically limited to the council? The answer is that rationally there are three possibilities: First, acceptance immediately after the offer is made obligatory; if he does not accept immediately, the offer becomes void. Second, acceptance is made dependent on beyond the council—meaning the buyer is given the option to accept in the council or after the council; in both cases, the sale is complete. Third, the option is kept until the end of the council; after the council ends, khiyar al-qabul does not remain. Among these three, in the first case there is difficulty for the buyer: he must answer immediately without consideration; he does not get an opportunity for thought. In the second case, i.e., keeping the option beyond the council, there is difficulty for the seller: he may have to wait a long time regarding whether the sale is concluded or not. In the third case, there is ease for both because the buyer gets the opportunity to think and reflect within the council, and the seller also does not have to wait too long. And the council brings together different things: for example, if someone recites a verse of prostration multiple times in one council, one prostration becomes obligatory. So to remove hardship from the seller and buyer and to create ease, all moments of the council are considered as one moment, and answering by the end of the council is like answering immediately after the offer. Therefore, khiyar al-qabul remains until the end of the council. The author of Hidayah says: Contracting a sale by letter and by messenger is like direct address. Even the council of receipt of the letter and delivery of the message is considered. For example, a person writes to someone: 'I have sold such-and-such slave to you for one thousand dirhams.' Then in the council in which he receives this letter, if he says 'I have bought' or 'I accept,' the sale is concluded. Or for example, the seller says to Khalid: 'I have sold such-and-such slave to Hamid for one thousand dirhams; you deliver this message to Hamid.' Then the messenger in whatever council he arrives and informs Hamid, if Hamid says in that council 'I have bought' or 'I accept,' the sale is concluded; otherwise, it is not concluded. The author of Hidayah says: The seller or buyer does not have the option to accept the sale for part of the goods. For example, if the seller says, 'I have sold these two items for one thousand rupees,' the buyer does not have the option to accept one of them for five hundred rupees. Similarly, if the buyer says, 'I have bought these two items for one thousand rupees,' the seller does not have the option to accept one of them for one thousand rupees. Likewise, the buyer does not have the option to accept the sale for part of the price. If the seller says, 'I have sold this slave to you for one thousand rupees,' and the buyer says, 'I have accepted for five hundred rupees,' it is not valid. The proof is that in these cases the transaction becomes divided. That is, the seller sold the slave for one thousand rupees, and the buyer accepted it for five hundred rupees, so the transaction changed, and the granter, i.e., the seller, is not pleased with a divided transaction. He is not pleased with division because in a divided transaction there is loss for the granter: when the seller sold something for one thousand rupees and the buyer accepted it for five hundred rupees, the seller suffers a loss of five hundred rupees. Or, for example, if the seller sold two items for one thousand rupees and the buyer accepted one of them for five hundred rupees, the seller's loss is that the custom of people is to mix inferior goods with good and sell them, and they reduce the price of good goods in order to sell the inferior goods.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the Hidayah, explaining the rulings related to khiyar al-qabul (option of acceptance) in sales. The author first clarifies the scenario: when one party (e.g., the seller) makes an offer (ijab), the other party (the buyer) has the option to accept or reject within the same council (majlis). This option is called khiyar al-qabul. The proof is rational: without this option, the buyer would be forced, losing the consent that is essential for a valid contract. The author then addresses an objection: even if the buyer does not acquire ownership or the right of ownership from the mere offer, he does acquire the 'right to become owner' (haqq al-tamalluk). Revoking the offer would therefore void this right. The answer is that the seller’s actual ownership (haqiqat al-milk) is stronger than the buyer’s mere right to become owner; thus, to preserve the stronger, the seller may revoke. An analogy is drawn with a father's right to own his child's property—the child’s actual ownership takes precedence over the father's right. Next, the author explains why khiyar al-qabul is limited to the end of the council. Three possibilities are considered: immediate acceptance (harsh for the buyer), acceptance at any future time (harsh for the seller), and acceptance only within the council (ease for both). The third is chosen, analogizing the council to a single moment in law. The author then states that writing and sending a messenger are like direct speech: the council of receipt is the valid council. Finally, the author rules that neither party may accept a partial offer (e.g., accepting one of two items or a reduced price) because that would constitute a different transaction that the offeror did not consent to, causing loss.

Key Terms
خیار قبول (khiyar al-qabul): The buyer's option to accept or reject the offer within the same council.
ایجاب (ijab): The first statement of offer in a contract.
قبول (qabul): The second statement of acceptance that concludes the contract.
مجلس (majlis): The meeting place where the offer and acceptance take place; legally, all moments are considered one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the buyer have the option to accept or reject within the council (khiyar al-qabul)?
A) To give the buyer time to find a better deal.
B) Because without this option, the buyer would be forced, and consent is necessary for a valid contract.
C) To allow the seller to find another buyer.
D) Because the council is a sacred space.

Key Takeaway
The buyer has the option to accept or reject until the end of the council; the seller may revoke before acceptance; partial acceptance is invalid unless each item's price is specified.

Rational Basis for Limiting Acceptance to the Session
Rational justification for limiting acceptance to the session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rational basis for the Hanafi rule that acceptance must occur within the contract session, and apply the analogy of sajdah al-tilawah to demonstrate how the session unifies discrete moments.

Core Idea
Acceptance is limited to the session to balance the buyer's need for deliberation with the seller's need for certainty.

The Hanafi school rules that acceptance must occur in the same session as the offer. But why this particular rule? The commentator presents a rational analysis: three possible structures for acceptance, each with different consequences for buyer and seller. The third option—confining acceptance to the session—is chosen because it provides ease (taysir) for both parties. The argument is reinforced through a legal analogy with sajdah al-tilawah, showing how the session unites what would otherwise be separate moments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three possible rules for acceptance timing
In your own words, list the three scenarios: (1) immediate acceptance or offer void, (2) acceptance allowed beyond the session, (3) acceptance valid only during the session. Note which party faces hardship in scenarios 1 and 2.
This sets up the problem that the Hanafi solution resolves.
2
Identify the third scenario as the Hanafi position
Recognize that the commentary adopts scenario 3: acceptance is valid anytime within the session but lapses when the session ends. This balances the buyer's right to deliberate with the seller's right to certainty.
This is the mufta bihi ruling.
3
Trace the rational argument (dalil aqli)
The commentator uses a rational argument based on ease (taysir). The first scenario burdens the buyer; the second burdens the seller. The third removes hardship from both. Why is this convincing? Because Islamic law aims to prevent hardship (haraj).
No naqli proof is cited; the ruling is established by prior evidence.
4
Analyse the analogy with sajdah al-tilawah
The commentator compares the contract session to a single unit for sajdah al-tilawah: multiple recitations in one session require only one sajdah. Similarly, all moments of the contract session are considered one moment. Thus acceptance at any point during the session is legally equivalent to immediate acceptance.
This is a qiyas shabahi (analogy based on similarity in legal effect).
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider: Buyer makes an offer. Seller thinks for 20 minutes while still seated, then says 'I accept.' According to this text, the sale is valid. If the seller stands up and leaves the room before accepting, the offer lapses. Confirm you understand the boundary.
This clarifies the condition of session continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد
ساتھ کیوں خاص کیا گیا ہے جواب عقلی طور پر تین صورتیں ہیں ایک تو یہ کہ ایجاب کے فورا بعد قبول کر نالازم قرار دیا جائے اگر فورا قبول نہ کرے تو ایجاب باطل ہو جائے ۔ دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ قبول کرنے کو ما وراء المجلس پر موقوف کیا جائے ۔ یعنی مشتری کو یہ اختیار دیا جائے کہ مجلس میں قبول کرے یا مجلس کے بعد قبول کرے دونوں صورتوں میں بیج تام ہو جائے گی ۔ تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ خیار کو آخر مجلس تک باقی رکھا جائے مجلس ختم ہونے کے بعد خیار قبول باقی نہ رہے ان تینوں صورتوں میں سے پہلی صورت میں مشتری کے لئے دشواری پیدا ہوگی ۔ بایں طور کہ اس کو بلا غور کئے ایجاب کے فوراً بعد جواب دینا پڑے گا غور فکر کا موقع نہیں ملے گا اور دوسری صورت یعنی خیار کو ماوراء مجلس پر موقوف رکھنے کی صورت میں بائع کے لئے دشواری پیدا ہوگی بایں طور کہ اس کو بیع کے تام ہونے یا نہ ہونے کے سلسلہ میں بہت مدت تک انتظار کرنا پڑ سکتا ہے۔ اور تیسری صورت میں دونوں کے لئے آسانی ہے کیونکہ مشتری کو بھی مجلس کے اندر غور و فکر کر نے کا موقع ملے گا۔ اور بائع کو بھی بہت زیادہ انتظار نہیں کرنا پڑیگا۔ اور مجلس متفرق چیزوں کو جمع بھی کر نیوالی ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے ایک مجلس میں آیت سجدہ کو بار بار پڑھا تو ایک ہی سجدہ واجب ہوگا پس بائع اور مشتری سے تنگی دور کرنے کے لئے اور آسانی کو پیدا کرنے کے لئے مجلس کی تمام ساعتوں کو بمنزلہ ایک ساعت کے اتار لیا گیا اور آخر مجلس تک جواب دینا ایجاب کے فوراً بعد جواب دینے کے مانند ہو گیا۔ اس لئے خیار قبول آخر مجلس تک باقی رہے گا۔
Why is it specifically tied to the session? The answer is that rationally there are three scenarios: First, that acceptance must be immediately after offer; if it is not immediate, the offer becomes void. Second, that acceptance is suspended pending beyond the session, i.e., the buyer is given the option to accept in the session or after it, and in both cases the sale is complete. Third, that the option remains until the end of the session, and after the session ends the option of acceptance no longer remains. Of these three scenarios, the first causes difficulty for the buyer, because he must respond immediately without reflection, having no opportunity for deliberation. The second scenario, suspending the option beyond the session, causes difficulty for the seller, because he may have to wait a long time regarding whether the sale is complete or not. The third scenario provides ease for both, because the buyer gets a chance to deliberate within the session, and the seller does not have to wait too long. And the session is something that gathers disparate things. For example, if someone recites an ayah of sajdah repeatedly in one session, only one sajdah becomes obligatory. Thus, to remove hardship from the seller and buyer and to create ease, all moments of the session are considered like one moment, and responding by the end of the session is like responding immediately after the offer. Therefore, the option of acceptance remains until the end of the session.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) explaining the Hanafi ruling that the option of acceptance (khiyar al-qabul) is limited to the session (majlis) of the contract. The commentator begins by presenting a rational analysis: there are three possible ways to structure acceptance. The first requires immediate acceptance, which is harsh on the buyer. The second extends the option indefinitely beyond the session, which is harsh on the seller. The third, which the Hanafi school adopts, balances both interests by confining acceptance to the contract session. The commentator reinforces this with a legal analogy: just as the requirement of sajdah al-tilawah is not multiplied by repeated recitation in one session, the session unifies what would otherwise be discrete moments. Thus, acceptance at any point during the session is legally equivalent to immediate acceptance. The ruling itself is from Zahir al-Riwayah: acceptance must occur in the same session; if the buyer stands up or leaves, the offer lapses.

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): The physical meeting place where the contracting parties are present and conduct the offer and acceptance.
خیار قبول (khiyar al-qabul): The option of the offeree to accept the offer; it remains active only during the contract session.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is acceptance limited to the contract session?
A) Because the Quran commands immediate acceptance
B) To balance the buyer's need for deliberation with the seller's need for certainty
C) Because the Prophet (SAW) never accepted delayed acceptance
D) Because the session is considered one moment for all legal acts

Key Takeaway
Acceptance is confined to the contract session to harmonise the buyer's right to reflect with the seller's right to prompt conclusion, and the session unifies all its moments into a single legal unit.
